<meta property="og:image" content="http://web.archive.org/web/20250621124810im_/https://www.ahajournals.org/cms/asset/7e98dc14-243d-424a-bc18-0ff6ee3b5ebe/strokeaha.123.042528.fig03.jpg"/>

            <meta property="og:image:width" content="2000"/>
            <meta property="og:image:height" content="1568"/>

<meta property="og:site_name" content="Stroke"/>

<meta property="og:description" content="  BACKGROUND: Dental caries is a highly prevalent disease worldwide. In the United States, untreated
dental caries is present in &amp;gt;1 in 5 adults. The objective of this study was to determine
the relationship between dental caries and incident ischemic stroke, coronary heart
disease (CHD) events, and death.   METHODS: The dental cohort (n=6351) of the ARIC study (Atherosclerosis Risk in Communities)
was followed for incident ischemic stroke, CHD event, and all-cause mortality. Of
all the participants at visit 4 (n=11 656), those who were unable to go through dental
examination, or with prevalent ischemic stroke and CHD events, were excluded. The
full-mouth dental examination was conducted at visit 4 (1996–1998), assessing dental
caries. The dose response of decayed, missing, and filled surfaces due to caries was
assessed and related to the outcome. Outcomes were assessed through the end of 2019.
Additionally, the effect of regular dental care utilization on dental caries was evaluated.   RESULTS: Participants with ≥1 dental caries had an increased risk of stroke (adjusted hazard
ratio [HR], 1.40 [95% CI, 1.10–1.79]) and death (adjusted HR, 1.13 [95% CI, 1.01–1.26])
but not for CHD events (adjusted HR, 1.13 [95% CI, 0.93–1.37]). The association of
dental caries and ischemic incident stroke was significantly higher in the African
American population compared with the White subgroup (interaction term P=0.0001). Increasing decayed, missing, and filled surfaces were significantly associated
with stroke (adjusted HR, 1.006 [95% CI, 1.001–1.011]) and death (adjusted HR, 1.003
[95% CI, 1.001–1.005]) but not CHD (adjusted HR, 1.002 [95% CI, 1.000–1.005]). Regular
dental care utilization lowered (adjusted odds ratio, 0.19 [95% CI, 0.16–0.22]; P&amp;lt;0.001) the chance of caries.   CONCLUSIONS: Among the cohort, dental caries was independently associated with the risk of ischemic
stroke and death, with the effect higher in African American participants. Regular
dental care utilization was associated with a lower chance of caries, emphasizing
its relevance in the prevention of these events.     Graphical Abstract "/>

target="_blank">https://orcid.org/0000-0002-9504-1256</a></span>, and <span property="author" typeof="Person" role="listitem"><a href="#con12"><span property="givenName">James</span> <span property="familyName">Beck</span>, <span property="honorificSuffix">PhD</span></a> <a class="orcid-id" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250621124810/https://orcid.org/0000-0001-5415-8311" property="identifier" aria-label="ORCID identifier" target="_blank">https://orcid.org/0000-0001-5415-8311</a></span></span></span><a href="#tab-contributors" class="to-authors-affiliations" data-id="article-authors-viewall">Author Info &amp; Affiliations</a></div><div class="core-self-citation"><div property="isPartOf" typeof="Periodical"><span property="name">Stroke</span></div><div class="core-enumeration"><a href="/web/20250621124810/https://www.ahajournals.org/toc/str/55/1"><span property="isPartOf" typeof="PublicationVolume">Volume <span data-core-nav="header" data-extent="frontmatter"><div class="core-nav-wrapper core-container" aria-label="Article navigation"><div><button data-id="article-toolbar-showhide" aria-controls="article_sections_menu" aria-expanded="false" aria-label="Toggle section navigation menu"><i class="icon-list" aria-hidden="true"></i><span>Contents</span></button><nav id="article_sections_menu" aria-label="Contents" data-core-nav="article"><ul><li><a href="#abstract">Abstract</a></li><li><a href="#graphical-abstract">Graphical Abstract</a></li><li><a href="#sec-1">METHODS</a></li><li><a href="#sec-2">RESULTS</a></li><li><a href="#sec-3">DISCUSSION</a></li><li><a href="#sec-4">ARTICLE INFORMATION</a></li><li><a href="#footnotes">Footnote</a></li><li><a href="#supplementary-materials">Supplemental Material</a></li><li><a href="#bibliography">REFERENCES</a></li></ul></nav></div><nav id="article_collateral_menu"><ul data-core-nav="collateral" class="collateral-pill"><li><a href="#core-collateral-info" data-id="article-nav-menubar-info" disabled="true" aria-disabled="true" title="Information &amp; Authors" tabindex="-1" data-toggle="tooltip" data-placement="left"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-info"></i><span class="sr-only">Information &amp; Authors</span></a></li><li><a href="#core-collateral-metrics" data-id="article-nav-menubar-metrics" disabled="true" aria-disabled="true" title="Metrics &amp; Citations" tabindex="-1" data-toggle="tooltip" data-placement="left"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-metrics"></i><span class="sr-only">Metrics &amp; Citations</span></a></li><li><a href="#core-collateral-fulltext-options" data-id="article-nav-menubar-metrics" disabled="true" aria-disabled="true" title="View Options" tabindex="-1" data-toggle="tooltip" data-placement="left"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-eye"></i><span class="sr-only">View Options</span></a></li><li><a href="#core-collateral-references" data-id="article-nav-menubar-references" disabled="true" aria-disabled="true" title="References" tabindex="-1" data-toggle="tooltip" data-placement="left"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-references"></i><span class="sr-only">References</span></a></li><li><a href="#core-collateral-figures" data-id="article-nav-menubar-figures" disabled="true" aria-disabled="true" title="Figures" tabindex="-1" data-toggle="tooltip" data-placement="left"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-photo"></i><span class="sr-only">Figures</span></a></li><li><a href="#core-collateral-tables" data-id="article-nav-menubar-tables" disabled="true" aria-disabled="true" title="Tables" tabindex="-1" data-toggle="tooltip" data-placement="left"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-table"></i><span class="sr-only">Tables</span></a></li><li><a href="#core-collateral-media" data-id="article-nav-menubar-media" disabled="true" aria-disabled="true" title="Media" tabindex="-1" data-toggle="tooltip" data-placement="left"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-play-circle-filled"></i><span class="sr-only">Media</span></a></li><li><a href="#core-collateral-share" data-id="article-nav-menubar-share" disabled="true" aria-disabled="true" title="Share" tabindex="-1" data-toggle="tooltip" data-placement="left"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-share"></i><span class="sr-only">Share</span></a></li></ul></nav></div></div><div data-core-wrapper="content"><div class="core-relations my-3"></div><div id="abstracts" data-extent="frontmatter"><div class="core-container"><section id="abstract" property="abstract" typeof="Text" role="doc-abstract"><h2 property="name">Abstract</h2><section id="abs-sec-1"><h3>BACKGROUND:</h3><div role="paragraph">Dental caries is a highly prevalent disease worldwide. In the United States, untreated dental caries is present in &gt;1 in 5 adults. The objective of this study was to determine the relationship between dental caries and incident ischemic stroke, coronary heart disease (CHD) events, and death.</div></section><section id="abs-sec-2"><h3>METHODS:</h3><div role="paragraph">The dental cohort (n=6351) of the ARIC study (Atherosclerosis Risk in Communities) was followed for incident ischemic stroke, CHD event, and all-cause mortality. Of all the participants at visit 4 (n=11 656), those who were unable to go through dental examination, or with prevalent ischemic stroke and CHD events, were excluded. The full-mouth dental examination was conducted at visit 4 (1996–1998), assessing dental caries. The dose response of decayed, missing, and filled surfaces due to caries was assessed and related to the outcome. Outcomes were assessed through the end of 2019. Additionally, the effect of regular dental care utilization on dental caries was evaluated.</div></section><section id="abs-sec-3"><h3>RESULTS:</h3><div role="paragraph">Participants with ≥1 dental caries had an increased risk of stroke (adjusted hazard ratio [HR], 1.40 [95% CI, 1.10–1.79]) and death (adjusted HR, 1.13 [95% CI, 1.01–1.26]) but not for CHD events (adjusted HR, 1.13 [95% CI, 0.93–1.37]). The association of dental caries and ischemic incident stroke was significantly higher in the African American population compared with the White subgroup (interaction term <i>P</i>=0.0001). Increasing decayed, missing, and filled surfaces were significantly associated with stroke (adjusted HR, 1.006 [95% CI, 1.001–1.011]) and death (adjusted HR, 1.003 [95% CI, 1.001–1.005]) but not CHD (adjusted HR, 1.002 [95% CI, 1.000–1.005]). Regular dental care utilization lowered (adjusted odds ratio, 0.19 [95% CI, 0.16–0.22]; <i>P</i>&lt;0.001) the chance of caries.</div></section><section id="abs-sec-4"><h3>CONCLUSIONS:</h3><div role="paragraph">Among the cohort, dental caries was independently associated with the risk of ischemic stroke and death, with the effect higher in African American participants. Regular dental care utilization was associated with a lower chance of caries, emphasizing its relevance in the prevention of these events.</div></section></section><section id="graphical-abstract" property="abstract" typeof="Text" role="doc-abstract"><h2 property="name">Graphical Abstract</h2><div class="figure-wrap"><figure id="fig0" class="graphic"><img src="/web/20250621124810im_/https://www.ahajournals.org/cms/10.1161/STROKEAHA.123.042528/asset/7e98dc14-243d-424a-bc18-0ff6ee3b5ebe/assets/graphic/strokeaha.123.042528.fig03.jpg" height="1568" width="2000" loading="lazy"/></figure></div></section></div></div><section id="bodymatter" data-extent="bodymatter" property="articleBody" typeof="Text"><div class="core-container"><div role="paragraph">Tooth loss is a marker of oral health that represents the end stage of oral diseases, such as dental caries and periodontal disease (PD). It is associated with incident cardiovascular diseases, including ischemic stroke, myocardial infarction, and all-cause mortality.<sup><a href="#R1" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R1">1</a></sup> Dental caries is a highly prevalent disease worldwide, ranking as the most common health condition according to the Global Burden of Disease in 2019, with 2 billion of the 3.5 billion people who experience oral diseases having dental caries of permanent teeth.<sup><a href="#R2" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R2">2</a></sup> A recent update in the US population reports that &gt;1 in 5 adults have untreated dental caries.<sup><a href="#R3" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R3">3</a></sup> Although both dental caries and PD can lead to tooth loss, they are distinct in their pathophysiology and their causative organisms. PD induces a chronic low-grade inflammation that damages the dental support tissues, whereas dental caries lead to oral bacteria penetrating the tooth structure, including dentin and enamel, causing chronic infection and inflammation of the dental pulp.<sup><a href="#R4" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R4">4</a>,<a href="#R5" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R5">5</a></sup> Inflammatory mediators from both conditions are released from the inflamed oral tissues, inducing a chronic systemic low-grade inflammatory response in the vascular system and subsequent atherosclerosis.<sup><a href="#R6" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R6">6</a></sup> Several types of PD pathogens have been detected in atheromatous plaques obtained from the carotid and coronary arteries.<sup><a href="#R7" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R7">7</a></sup> The principal dental caries pathogen, <i>Streptococcus mutans</i>, has been detected in heart valve and atheromatous plaque specimens.<sup><a href="#R8" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R8">8</a></sup> This evidence indicates that individual oral conditions may be a risk factor for coronary heart disease (CHD) events.</div><div role="paragraph">PD has been shown to be associated independently with incident cardiovascular diseases, including ischemic stroke, myocardial infarction, and all-cause mortality.<sup><a href="#R9" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R9">9</a></sup> However, there are limited data on the association between dental caries and an incident CHD event.<sup><a href="#R10" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R10">10</a></sup> We hypothesized that dental caries is predictive of ischemic stroke, CHD events, and death in the ARIC study (Atherosclerosis Risk in Communities). Based on the same dental cohort of the ARIC (DARIC) data set, we reported an association between PD and incident atrial fibrillation (AF),<sup><a href="#R11" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R11">11</a></sup> which, in turn, increased the risk of systemic ischemic events by ≈3×.<sup><a href="#R12" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R12">12</a></sup> Similarly, periodontitis or dental plaque has been associated with incident AF.<sup><a href="#R13" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R13">13</a></sup> We investigated the association between dental caries and incident AF and whether AF mediates associations between caries and ischemic events.</div><div role="paragraph">It has been reported that regular dental care use was associated with 23% lower rates of ischemic stroke compared with episodic dental care users.<sup><a href="#R14" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R14">14</a></sup> Additionally, we propose to test whether regular dental care use will be associated with lower levels of dental caries compared with episodic dental care use.</div><section id="sec-1" data-type="methods"><h2>METHODS</h2><section id="sec-1-1"><h3>Study Population</h3><div role="paragraph">The authors declare that all supporting data are available within the article, and the ARIC investigators are open to provide the data used in this article to a researcher, allowing them to replicate the findings, contingent upon the execution of a data use agreement that guarantees the safeguarding of the confidentiality of ARIC participants’ data. Starting in 1987, the ARIC study began its cohort to examine the cause of atherosclerosis and its associated outcomes.<sup><a href="#R15" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R15">15</a></sup> Participants for this cohort were enrolled from 4 states in the United States, including participants from North Carolina, Minnesota, Mississippi, and Maryland. The baseline dental examination was conducted at visit 4 (1996–1998), and outcomes were assessed at follow-up through the end of 2019. Vascular risk factors for this analysis were assessed during visit 4 (1996–1998). Hospital medical records were obtained for any hospitalizations occurring over the median follow-up period of 21 years. The study team also contacted the ARIC study participants every year to evaluate for stroke, CHD events, or all-cause mortality.<sup><a href="#R15" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R15">15</a></sup> All participants provided written informed consent. The 4 examination field centers and the coordinating center were granted human subject approvals.</div><div role="paragraph">At visit 4, 11 656 individuals were assessed. Of those participants, 6744 dental subjects were enrolled in DARIC (1317 refused oral examination; 1895 had medical contraindications to a dental exam at the time [contraindications include a dentist or physician stating individual needed to take antibiotics before every dental visit, congenital heart disease, rheumatic heart disease, heart murmur, endocarditis, mitral valve prolapse, pacemaker, heart, kidney, or other organ transplants, artificial heart valve, stent, shunt, or artificial joint, kidney dialysis, major surgery, radiation, or chemotherapy within the last 2 months, taking prednisone or an immunosuppressive medication]; and 1619 were edentulous [total tooth loss, comprising the major reasons for exclusion]).<sup><a href="#R16" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R16">16</a></sup> Of these, 6351 study participants without prior CHD or ischemic stroke had complete covariate information and underwent full-mouth periodontal assessment of 6 sites per tooth, as well as a dental caries exam. Individuals not of the African American or White race were excluded (due to the limited sample size of other races).<sup><a href="#R16" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R16">16</a></sup> The individuals included in the study were compared with those excluded from the study.</div></section><section id="sec-1-2"><h3>Assessment of Dental Caries and PD</h3><div role="paragraph">Participants underwent full-mouth caries assessments at visit 4 using modified Radike criteria.<sup><a href="#R17" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R17">17</a></sup> The modifications consisted of using a tongue blade instead of an explorer, which made the examination more visual. The number of coronal and root carious lesions was recorded separately. For each tooth, a numerical status was recorded and denoted if a tooth was sound, decayed or filled, missing, restored with a crown, a decayed root fragment, a sound root fragment, or an implant. If the tooth status was decayed, filled, or restored with a crown, then it would be coded as dental surface caries and dental root caries. Root caries was recorded if there was a discrete, well-defined, and discolored cavitation on the root surface and the tongue blade could scrape away some of the enamel. Tooth fractures, erosion, and abrasions were considered distinct and were not recorded as caries. Participants with lesions, but not frank caries, were further investigated to detect pit, fissure, and smooth surface lesions, as well as root and coronal caries. The presence of coronal caries was used as the primary exposure of interest. To further explore the dose response of dental caries to our outcomes, decayed, missing, and filled surfaces (DMFSs) due to caries index were assessed.<sup><a href="#R18" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R18">18</a></sup></div><div role="paragraph">The presence or absence of PD was also assessed as a covariate during visit 4 within the DARIC study. The periodontal profile class was classified on a scale from periodontal profile class-A (periodontal health) or periodontal profile class-B through periodontal profile class-G (severe PD).<sup><a href="#R19" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R19">19</a></sup></div></section><section id="sec-1-3"><h3>Ischemic Stroke, CHD Event, and Death</h3><div role="paragraph">In the ARIC study, cases were identified through annual contact of participants and searching discharge lists from local hospitals. Deaths were ascertained through the review of hospital discharge records, death certificates, and informant interviews or physician questionnaires for out-of-hospital deaths. Incident CHD events included fatal CHD, definite or probable myocardial infarction, and silent CHD (as determined by EKGs). Definite or probable hospitalized embolic or thrombotic strokes were determined from diagnosis codes (<i>International Classification of Diseases, Ninth Revision</i>, codes 430–437), hospital records, and neuroimaging reports. Eligible ischemic stroke cases were classified by a computer algorithm and a physician reviewer, and differences were adjudicated based on the event criteria adapted from the National Survey of Stroke.<sup><a href="#R20" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R20">20</a></sup> Follow-up information was available through 2019. All potential CHD and stroke events were classified as definite, probable, or suspected according to standardized criteria.<sup><a href="#R21" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R21">21</a>,<a href="#R22" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R22">22</a></sup> Incident CHD events, ischemic stroke, and death that occurred after dental assessment on visit 4 were included in the analysis of outcomes.</div></section><section id="sec-1-4"><h3>Assessment of Incident AF</h3><div role="paragraph">AF was assessed by study EKGs, hospital discharge diagnosis codes, and death certificates.<sup><a href="#R11" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R11">11</a></sup> Standard 10-s, 12-lead EKGs were obtained at baseline and each of the subsequent follow-up examinations. Tracings were transmitted electronically to the ARIC EKG Reading Center (Epidemiological Cardiology Research Center, Wake Forest School of Medicine, Winston-Salem, NC), where they underwent automated reading and coding. Tracings with AF were reviewed by a cardiologist. AF was identified from hospitalizations or death certificates using the <i>International Classification of Diseases, Ninth Revision</i>, Clinical Modification code 427.31 or 427.32. The methodology has high sensitivity and specificity.<sup><a href="#R11" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R11">11</a></sup> For analysis of data related to incident AF, those with AF detected before visit 4 (n=90, 1.4%) were excluded.</div></section><section id="sec-1-5"><h3>Assessment of Dental Care Utilization</h3><div role="paragraph">Dental care utilization was classified using participant-reported responses to the administered dental history questionnaire. Subjects were classified as regular users (those who sought routine dental care) or episodic users (those who sought dental care only when symptomatic).</div></section><section id="sec-1-6"><h3>Statistical Analysis</h3><div role="paragraph">The cohort consisted of 6351 participants assessed for dental caries at visit 4. Demographics and covariates, including age, sex, race, body mass index (BMI), hypertension, diabetes, education, smoking status, alcohol use, and cholesterol, were assessed using a <i>t</i> test for continuous variables and a χ2 test for categorical variables. Triglycerides and serum CRP (C-reactive protein) are not normally distributed and were assessed using a nonparametric Mann-Whitney <i>U</i> test. Gray test was run to determine differences in the time-to-event distribution for incident ischemic stroke, CHD event, and death between the dental caries and no dental caries groups. The proportional hazards assumption was tested using interaction terms between time measures and covariates in the model. Cox proportional hazards models were then used to assess crude, followed by adjusted, hazard ratios (HRs) and 95% CIs of incident ischemic stroke, CHD events, and death, from the initial dental assessment. Following the crude HR analyses, stratified analyses and tests for interaction were run using individual covariates in the crude model. The interaction term and stratified analysis for ischemic stroke, CHD events, and all-cause mortality are depicted in <a href="#F1">Figure 1A through 1C</a>, respectively. The associations between dental caries and ischemic stroke, CHD events, and death were adjusted for covariates that did not meet the criteria for significant interaction. Those with significant interaction on stratified analysis are reported as effect modifiers, such as race, and were not included in the model as confounders. Because all-cause mortality would be a competing risk for both CHD event and ischemic stroke, we used the Fine and Gray method to estimate multivariable-adjusted subdistribution relative hazards of ischemic stroke and CHD event, respectively, while adjusting for the same covariates as listed above.<sup><a href="#R23" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R23">23</a></sup> Our data may have unmeasured confounders (eg, dietary factors associated with caries). As such, we assessed the sensitivity of the caries results in fitted survival models to the unmeasured confounders.<sup><a href="#R24" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R24">24</a></sup></div><div class="figure-wrap"><figure id="F1" class="graphic"><img src="/web/20250621124810im_/https://www.ahajournals.org/cms/10.1161/STROKEAHA.123.042528/asset/e370d096-f8cd-49ee-9e7b-5b80873532cb/assets/graphic/strokeaha.123.042528.fig01.jpg" height="5406" width="2400" aria-labelledby="F1" loading="lazy"/><figcaption><span class="heading">Figure 1</span>. <b>Subgroup analyses for interaction of dental surface caries. A</b>, Incident ischemic stroke; (<b>B</b>), incident coronary heart disease; (<b>C</b>), death from all causes. HR indicates hazard ratio; and LDL, low-density lipoprotein. *<i>P</i> value is the test of interaction between caries and each subgroup.</figcaption></figure></div><div role="paragraph">By using structural equation modeling of the Baron and Kenny approach, mediation analysis was performed to determine the role of incident AF in mediating the effect of caries on incident ischemic stroke. Additionally, the proportion mediated was computed from the difference in the natural direct and natural indirect effects.<sup><a href="#R25" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R25">25</a></sup> Finally, we assessed whether regular dental care use reduced the chances of developing dental caries on univariate (odds ratio analysis and χ<sup>2</sup> test) and multivariable logistic regression analysis. All the data analyses for this study were conducted using SAS, version 9.4 (SAS Institute).</div></section></section><section id="sec-2" data-type="results"><h2>RESULTS</h2><div role="paragraph">At visit 4, individuals included in the DARIC study (n=6351) were compared with those excluded (n=5305) from the study (<a href="#T1">Table 1</a>). Of the 6351 participants assessed in DARIC at visit 4 without prior stroke or CHD events, 1171 were found to have dental caries, and 5180 were noted without caries (<a href="#T2">Table 2</a>). The group of subjects with dental caries was significantly younger, had a higher proportion of males compared with females, was more likely to be African American than White, and had a higher mean BMI and waist-to-hip ratio compared with those without dental caries. They were also more likely to have hypertension, diabetes, and PD and more likely to be current smokers. On the opposite end of the scale, they were also less likely to have intermediate or advanced education and less likely to be current alcohol users. Additionally, the dental caries group had a higher low-density lipoprotein (LDL), high sensitivity CRP, and a lower median triglyceride level compared with those without dental caries.</div><div class="figure-wrap"><figure id="T1" class="table"><figcaption><span class="heading">Table 1</span>. Characteristics of the Study Participants According to Inclusion/Exclusion in DARIC Visit 4</figcaption><div class="table-wrap"><table data-shading="custom"><thead><tr data-xml-align="left"><td (87–176)</td><td>0.20</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="notes"><div role="doc-footnote">All remaining proportion (%). DARIC indicates dental cohort of the Atherosclerosis Risk in Communities; HDL, high-density lipoprotein; and LDL, low-density lipoprotein.</div><div role="doc-footnote" data-has="label"><div class="label">*</div><div id="tab1fn2" role="paragraph">Mean+SD.</div></div><div role="doc-footnote" data-has="label"><div class="label">†</div><div id="tab1fn3" role="paragraph">Media (interquartile range).</div></div></div></figure></div><div class="figure-wrap"><figure id="T2" class="table"><figcaption><span class="heading">Table 2</span>. mg/dL<a href="#tab2fn2" role="doc-noteref">*</a></td><td>201.3±35.2</td><td>201.1±39.0</td><td>0.88</td></tr><tr><td> LDL<a href="#tab2fn2" (81–161)</td><td>&lt;0.001</td></tr><tr><td>High-sensitivity CRP, mg/dL<a href="#tab2fn3" role="doc-noteref">†</a></td><td>2.6 (0.9–6.8)</td><td>3.2 (1.2–7.6)</td><td>0.001</td></tr><tr><td>Periodontal disease</td><td>67.3</td><td>94.0</td><td>&lt;0.001</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="notes"><div role="doc-footnote">All remaining proportion (%). CRP indicates C-reactive protein; HDL, high-density lipoprotein; and LDL, low-density lipoprotein.</div><div role="doc-footnote" data-has="label"><div class="label">*</div><div id="tab2fn2" role="paragraph">Mean±SD.</div></div><div role="doc-footnote" data-has="label"><div class="label">†</div><div id="tab2fn3" role="paragraph">Median (interquartile range).</div></div></div></figure></div><div role="paragraph">During a median of a 21-year follow-up period, the group with dental caries was associated with higher cumulative incidence of ischemic stroke (<a href="#F2">Figure 2A</a>; Gray test, <i>P</i>&lt;0.0001), higher cumulative incidence of CHD events (<a href="#F2">Figure 2B</a>; Gray test, <i>P</i>=0.0187), and higher cumulative incidence of all-cause mortality (<a href="#F2">Figure 2C</a>; Gray test, <i>P</i>&lt;0.0001) compared with the group without dental caries. Of note, the cumulative incidence of ischemic stroke in the dental caries group was higher than the no caries group after an initial 3- to 5-year period after visit 4. However, dental caries still met the proportional hazards assumption using the time interaction criteria.</div><div class="figure-wrap"><figure id="F2" class="graphic"><img src="/web/20250621124810im_/https://www.ahajournals.org/cms/10.1161/STROKEAHA.123.042528/asset/98dd38f4-aaf5-43d5-8828-a1825989f7e2/assets/graphic/strokeaha.123.042528.fig02.jpg" height="1851" width="2400" aria-labelledby="F2" loading="lazy"/><figcaption><span class="heading">Figure 2</span>. <b> Cumulative incident function of event with or without evidence of dental caries. A</b>, Incident ischemic stroke; (<b>B</b>), coronary heart disease (CHD) event; (<b>C</b>), death.</figcaption></figure></div><div role="paragraph">During the same 21-year follow-up period, incident ischemic stroke was noted in 113 (10%) of the 1171 participants with dental caries and 328 (6%) of 5180 without dental caries (<a href="#T3">Table 3</a>). Univariable Cox regression analysis showed a crude HR of 1.70 (95% CI, 1.37–2.10). In addition, an incident ischemic CHD event was noted in 163 (14%) of 1171 with dental caries and 634 (12%) of 5180 participants without dental caries. Univariable Cox regression analysis showed a crude HR of 1.23 (95% CI, 1.03–1.46). During a median 21-year follow-up period, incident all-cause mortality was noted in 534 (46%) of 1171 with dental caries and 2051 (40%) of 5180 without dental caries. Univariable Cox regression analysis showed a crude HR of 1.24 (95% CI, 1.12–1.36). In model 1, HR was adjusted for the covariates age, sex, hypertension, diabetes, BMI, LDL cholesterol, smoking status, 3-level educational status, and the presence of PD. Dental caries was significantly associated with ischemic stroke (adjusted HR, 1.40 [95% CI, 1.10–1.79]) and all-cause mortality (adjusted HR, 1.13 [95% CI, 1.01–1.26]) but not CHD event (adjusted HR, 1.13 [95% CI, 0.93–1.37]). Because all-cause mortality would be a competing risk for both CHD events and ischemic stroke, in model 2, we report a Fine and Gray subdistribution HR that included death as an additional event type, adjusting for the same covariates as listed above. Dental caries was significantly associated with ischemic stroke (adjusted HR, 1.19 [95% CI, 1.06–1.33]) and CHD event (adjusted HR, 1.16 [95% CI, 1.02–1.32]). Full model information is included in <a href="#supplementary-materials">Table S1A through S1C</a>.</div><div class="figure-wrap"><figure id="T3" class="table"><figcaption><span class="heading">Table 3</span>. HRs and 95% CI for the Association of the Presence of Dental Caries, Incident Ischemic Stroke, CHD Event (CHD Death and Myocardial Infarction), and Death (All-Cause Mortality)</figcaption><div class="table-wrap"><table data-shading="custom"><thead><tr data-xml-align="left"><th colspan="3"><span>Dental caries</span></th><th colspan="3"><span>Unadjusted</span></th><th colspan="3"><span>Model 1 (Cox proportional hazards)</span></th><th colspan="3"><span>Model 2 (Fine and Gray adjusted subdistribution)</span></th></tr><tr data-xml-align="left"><td> </td><th>Absent (n=5180)</th><th>Present (n=1171)</th><th>Crude HR</th><th>95% CI</th><th><i>P</i> values</th><th>Adjusted HR<a href="#tab3fn2" role="doc-noteref">*</a></th><th>95% CI</th><th><i>P</i> values</th><th>Adjusted HR<a href="#tab3fn3" role="doc-noteref">†</a></th><th>95% CI</th><th><i>P</i> values</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td> Stroke</td><td>328</td><td>113</td><td>1.70</td><td>1.37–2.10</td><td>&lt;0.001</td><td>1.40</td><td>1.10–1.79</td><td>0.01</td><td>1.19</td><td>1.06–1.33</td><td>0.003</td></tr><tr><td>CHD event</td><td>634</td><td>163</td><td>1.23</td><td>1.03–1.46</td><td>0.02</td><td>1.13</td><td>0.93–1.37</td><td>0.22</td><td>1.16</td><td>1.03–1.32</td><td>0.02</td></tr><tr><td> Death</td><td>2051</td><td>534</td><td>1.24</td><td>1.12–1.36</td><td>&lt;0.001</td><td>1.13</td><td>1.01–1.26</td><td>0.04</td><td> </td><td>…</td><td> </td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="notes"><div role="doc-footnote">BMI indicates body mass index; CHD, coronary heart disease; HR, hazard ratio; and LDL, low-density lipoprotein.</div><div role="doc-footnote" data-has="label"><div class="label">*</div><div id="tab3fn2" role="paragraph">Adjusted for age, sex, hypertension, diabetes, BMI, LDL cholesterol, smoking status, 3-level educational status, and the presence of periodontal disease.</div></div><div role="doc-footnote" data-has="label"><div class="label">†</div><div id="tab3fn3" role="paragraph">Fine and Gray adjusted subdistribution HR that included death as a competing event, also adjusted for age, sex, hypertension, diabetes, BMI, LDL cholesterol, smoking status, 3-level educational status, and the presence of periodontal disease.</div></div></div></figure></div><div role="paragraph">The association of dental caries and ischemic incident stroke was significantly higher in the African American population (HR, 2.89 [95% CI, 1.82–4.59]) compared with the White subgroup (HR, 0.96 [95% CI, 0.67–1.38]; <a href="#F1">Figure 1A</a>). On the introduction of the interaction terms of dental caries and race group into the Cox regression model, the interaction term was statistically significant (<i>P</i>=0.0001), suggestive of effect modification. The interaction remained significant after adding the remaining covariates as confounders in the model (<i>P</i>=0.0001). The interaction for other covariates remained nonsignificant. While the association between race and CHD event was similar across both races (<a href="#F1">Figure 1B</a>), the African American subgroup had a stronger association with all-cause mortality (HR, 1.54 [95% CI, 1.39–1.89]) compared with the White subgroup (HR, 1.20 [95% CI, 1.05–1.37]) with the interaction term achieving statistical significance (<i>P</i>=0.03; <a href="#F1">Figure 1C</a>). The association of dental caries and incident all-cause mortality was significantly higher in the no diabetes group (HR, 1.32 [95% CI, 1.18–1.47]) compared with the diabetes subgroup (HR, 1.02 [95% CI, 0.83–1.25]). On the introduction of the interaction terms of dental caries and diabetes into the Cox regression model, the interaction term was statistically significant (<i>P</i>=0.03). The interactions for all other covariates were statistically nonsignificant.</div><div role="paragraph">The severity of dental caries was gauged by the presence of DMFS (median, 68; interquartile range, 50–87). Increasing DMFS was significantly associated with ischemic stroke (crude HR, 1.008 [95% CI, 1.004–1012]), CHD events (crude HR, 1.006 [95% CI, 1.004–1.008]), and all-cause mortality (crude HR, 1.007 [95% CI, 1.005–1.008]), suggestive of a dose response of DMFS to outcome (<a href="#supplementary-materials">Figure S1</a>). The association remained significant for ischemic stroke (adjusted HR, 1.006 [95% CI, 1.001–1.011]) and all-cause mortality (adjusted HR, 1.003 [95% CI, 1.001–1.005]) after adjusting for the covariates age, sex, hypertension, diabetes, BMI, LDL cholesterol, smoking status, 3-level educational status, and PD. The sensitivity of caries results in fitted survival models to the unmeasured confounder is shown in <a href="#supplementary-materials">Table S2</a>. To fully mediate the association of caries, an unmeasured confounder must have an HR of at least 3.0 (assuming a difference of at least 30% of prevalence of the confounder between caries and no caries groups) to result in a nonsignificant association between caries and ischemic risk.</div><div role="paragraph">Using structural equation modeling (Baron and Kenny approach), we found that incident AF is not a likely mediator of dental caries–ischemic stroke association (<a href="#supplementary-materials">Figure S2</a>). Dental caries was not associated with incident AF (HR, 1.03 [95% CI, 0.88–1.19]). In the mediated model, the effect of dental caries on ischemic stroke (HR, 1.75 [95% CI, 1.40–2.18]) was similar to the effect in the unmediated model (HR, 1.74 [95% CI, 1.40–2.15]). The proportion mediated was small and not significant (0.03; <i>P</i>=0.43).</div></section><section id="sec-3" data-type="discussion"><h2>DISCUSSION</h2><div role="paragraph">The prevalence of dental caries noted in our study is ≈1 in 5 reported in the US adult population.<sup><a href="#R3" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R3">3</a></sup> We report an independent association between dental caries and both ischemic stroke and all-cause mortality. The association remained significant upon the use of an adjusted subdistribution model accounting for death as a competing event. Additionally, we report associations between DMFS and outcomes, with significant independent associations noted between DMFS and ischemic stroke, as well as all-cause mortality. Case-control studies that measured the number of decayed, missing, and filled teeth as a surrogate of dental caries show mixed results, with some showing an association and a possible dose response with ischemic stroke,<sup><a href="#R26" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R26">26</a>,<a href="#R27" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R27">27</a></sup> whereas others failed to find a significant association.<sup><a href="#R28" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R28 R29 R30 R31">28–31</a></sup> A recent meta-analysis of pooled data showed a significantly higher mean decayed, missing, and filled teeth score among patients with ischemic stroke.<sup><a href="#R32" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R32">32</a></sup> Following the meta-analysis, an analysis of data from the Third National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey showed that the number of missing teeth was independently associated with self-reported history of stroke.<sup><a href="#R33" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R33">33</a></sup> The association with dental caries was statistically nonsignificant. Recent reports from the large Korean nationwide cohort study found a dose-dependent association between tooth loss (a marker of both PD and caries) and incident myocardial infarction, heart failure, ischemic stroke, and all-cause death.<sup><a href="#R34" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R34">34</a></sup> After a median follow-up of 9.5 years, the risk of major CHD events was higher when a subject had a higher number of dental caries, PD, or more tooth loss.<sup><a href="#R35" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R35">35</a></sup> This study did not investigate the individual categories of CHD events separately. The study also reported that ≥1 tooth brushing sessions a day were associated with a 9% significantly lower risk of CHD events. Once a year or more dental visits for cleaning were also shown to reduce CHD risk by 14%. In line with these findings, we have reported in the past that regular dental care was associated with 23% lower rates of ischemic stroke compared with regular dental care users after adjustment for covariates.<sup><a href="#R11" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R11">11</a></sup> We now report that regular dental care users have a significantly lower risk of dental caries compared with episodic dental care users. The finding that regular dental care by lowering the risk of dental caries may aid in stroke prevention emphasizes the importance of oral health in maintaining cerebrovascular health.</div><div role="paragraph">We observed a racial disparity in the prevalence of caries. Prior studies have noted disparities in the prevalence of untreated decayed teeth between African American (42%) and White (22%) populations.<sup><a href="#R35" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R35">35</a></sup> This disparity is partially explained by sociocultural differences between racial groups. African American individuals are less likely to have access to and use oral health care means.<sup><a href="#R36" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R36">36</a></sup> Other factors contributing to the disparities in oral care include differences in oral health education, socioeconomic status, and transmission of cariogenic bacteria. Caries may be a contributing factor to the higher risk of ischemic stroke noted among young African American individuals.<sup><a href="#R37" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R37">37</a></sup> Stroke continues to disproportionately affect African American individuals.<sup><a href="#R38" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R38">38</a></sup> Stroke racial disparities are not entirely explained by traditional stroke risk factors. The REGARDS study (Reasons for Geographic and Racial Differences in Stroke) found that tooth loss due to PD was more common among African American participants than White participants, and tooth loss was associated with higher stroke risk and stroke risk factors in the stroke belt.<sup><a href="#R39" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R39">39</a></sup> Given these 2 associations, REGARDS investigators proposed that PD may be contributing to the racial disparity in stroke. Additional results from this study show that low socioeconomic status is associated with greater tooth loss and is seen more frequently in the African American population as opposed to their White counterparts.<sup><a href="#R39" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R39">39</a></sup></div><div role="paragraph">In addition to the racial disparity in the prevalence of caries, we noted significant interaction in both caries-stroke and caries-death associations. The reason for these interactions in the adult population needs further investigation. Diabetes had a negative interaction with all-cause mortality, as there was a stronger association between caries and death noted among those without diabetes (HR, 1.32 [95% CI, 1.18–1.47]) compared with the diabetes subgroup (HR, 1.02 [95% CI, 0.83–1.25]). This may have been driven by the proportion of non-CHD mortality in the no diabetes group, included within all-cause mortality.</div><div role="paragraph">As we did not find a mediator role of incident AF in the association between dental caries and ischemic stroke, it remains unknown whether other mechanisms may be at play. <i>S mutans</i>, the primary pathogen causing dental caries, was detected at higher frequencies and quantities than the periodontal pathogens in both heart valve tissues obtained during heart valve surgery and atheromatous plaque samples obtained from aortic samples during CHD surgeries.<sup><a href="#R8" role="doc-biblioref" data-xml-rid="R8">8</a></sup> Due to this finding, it is possible to speculate that <i>S mutans</i> in dental caries may be responsible for ischemic stroke via cardioembolism (infective endocarditis) and atherothrombosis and risk factors (eg, diabetes) of the cerebral vasculature. This may also explain the 3- to 5-year delayed increase in the cumulative incidence of ischemic stroke in the dental caries group noted in <a href="#F1">Figure 1A</a>.</div><div role="paragraph">Few limitations were observed in our study. The individuals included in the study were younger and had lower BMI, less prevalent hypertension, diabetes, and higher levels of education. This may be attributed to older people with overall bad oral health who had been excluded due to tooth loss. Other limitations of the study include a single dental assessment and the use of education level only as a surrogate for socioeconomic status. Limitations also include residual and unmeasured confounding that might be present despite trying to account for potential confounders in the statistical analysis. To address the issue of unmeasured confounders, we conducted a sensitivity analysis to test its effect on the association between caries and ischemic stroke. To fully mediate the association of caries, the unmeasured confounder must have an HR of at least 3.0, an unlikely scenario. Despite this, the strength of our study includes its large community-based cohort, long follow-up duration, adjudication of outcome events, and assessment of multiple confounders, including PD. Because PD is associated with dental caries, it may be difficult to disentangle the independent effect of caries specifically on cardiovascular outcomes.</div><div role="paragraph">We think that this may be one of the first studies to evaluate the association between dental caries and incident ischemic stroke within a large US community-based cohort. Regular dental use is associated with lower levels of dental caries, suggesting that it may be a means of lowering this risk for incident ischemic stroke.</div></section><section id="sec-4"><h2>ARTICLE INFORMATION</h2><section id="sec-4-2"><h3>Supplemental Material</h3><div role="paragraph">Tables S1–S2</div><div role="paragraph">Figures S1–S2</div></section></section></div></section><section id="backmatter" data-extent="backmatter"><div class="core-container"><section id="footnotes"><h2>Footnote</h2><div role="doc-footnote"><section><h3>Nonstandard Abbreviations and Acronyms</h3><dl><div><dt>AF</dt><dd>atrial fibrillation</dd></div><div><dt>ARIC</dt><dd>Atherosclerosis Risk in Communities</dd></div><div><dt>BMI</dt><dd>body mass index</dd></div><div><dt>CHD</dt><dd>coronary heart disease</dd></div><div><dt>DARIC</dt><dd>dental cohort of the Atherosclerosis Risk in Communities</dd></div><div><dt>DMFS</dt><dd>decayed, missing, and filled surface</dd></div><div><dt>HR</dt><dd>hazard ratio</dd></div><div><dt>PD</dt><dd>periodontal disease</dd></div><div><dt>REGARDS</dt><dd>Reasons for Geographic and Racial Differences in Stroke</dd></div></dl></section></div></section><section id="supplementary-materials" <section id="comments"><div class="comments__wrapper__header"><div class="article__body"><h2 class="comments__title"><span>eLetters</span><span class="comments__count">(0)</span></h2></div><p>eLetters should relate to an article recently published in the journal and are not a forum for providing unpublished data. Comments are reviewed for appropriate use of tone and language. Comments are not peer-reviewed. Acceptable comments are posted to the journal website only. Comments are not published in an issue and are not indexed in PubMed. Comments should be no longer than 500 words and will only be posted online. References are limited to 10. Authors of the article cited in the comment will be invited to reply, as appropriate.</p><p>Comments and feedback on AHA/ASA Scientific Statements and Guidelines should be directed to the AHA/ASA Manuscript Oversight Committee via its <a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250621124810/https://professional.heart.org/en/guidelines-and-statements/correspondence" target="_blank">Correspondence</a> page.</p><div class="comments__add-comment"><a href="/web/20250621124810/https://www.ahajournals.org/action/ssostart?idp=https://ahasso.heart.org&amp;redirectUri=/doi/full/10.1161/STROKEAHA.123.042528" class="btn btn--primary"><svg width="14" height="14" viewbox="0 0 14 14" fill="none" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" focusable="false"><path d="M12.333 0.333252H1.66634C0.929675 0.333252 0.333008 0.929919 0.333008 1.66659V13.6666L2.99967 10.9999H12.333C13.0697 10.9999 13.6663 10.4033 13.6663 9.66659V1.66659C13.6663 0.929919 13.0697 0.333252 12.333 0.333252Z" fill="white"></path></svg><span>Sign In to Submit a Response to This Article</span></a></div></div></section>
